The most prominent examples include:\n\n1. Calixarenes: Especially the upper-rim functionalized calix[4]arenes, which present a bowl-shaped aromatic cavity ideal for binding alkali metal cations (such as K+, Na+, Cs+) via cation-π interactions. The aromatic rings of the calixarene provide a strong π-system that stabilizes the cationic guest.\n\n2. Cyclophanes: These are macrocyclic hosts with two or more aromatic rings held in close proximity, creating a π-rich environment that can encapsulate cations, particularly ammonium ions and alkali metals, through cation-π interactions.\n\n3. Cucurbiturils: While cucurbiturils are often associated with ion-dipole interactions, certain derivatives and binding modes can involve significant cation-π contributions, especially with aromatic portals.\n\n4. Pillararenes: These hosts have a rigid, pillar-shaped structure with multiple aromatic rings aligned in parallel, creating a cavity that can bind cations via cation-π interactions.\n\n5. Cryptophanes: These cage-like hosts, composed of aromatic units, can encapsulate cations and stabilize them through cation-π interactions.\n\nIn all these cases, the cation-π interaction is a key driving force for guest binding, often leading to high selectivity for specific cations. The strength and selectivity of binding depend on the size, shape, and electronic properties of both the host and the guest.\n\nReferences: For further reading, see reviews on cation-π interactions in supramolecular chemistry, such as Chem. Cryptophanes: These cage-like hosts, composed of aromatic units, can encapsulate cations and stabilize them through cation-π interactions.\n\nIn all these cases, the cation-π interaction is a key driving force for guest binding, often leading to high selectivity for specific cations.
